Karnataka Watershed Development Project
15-1
Monitoring & Evaluation
15.0 MONITORING AND EVALUATION
Provides various particpatory evaluation and monitoring methods to be adopted for
effective project management and to ensure the meshes of planned progress through
various environmental and social indicators. Emphasis has been on community
participation for M & E.
15.1 Monitoring And Evaluation As A Management System
In watershed management, a set of baselines should be established to act as starting points
by which progress can be evaluated right from the planning stage itself. It is important that
the baselines are set from the people's perspective, since they are to be involved in
monitoring their own progress.  The identification and adoption of indicators by which
progress is to be measured (or monitored) are then defined. Decisions also have to be made
on how and by whom information is to be obtained to measure progress against these
criteria. Indicators measure progress of planned activities.
Monitoring is therefore the systematic measurement of a project's progress using these
indicators, with periodic analyses of progress to see what could have been done more
efficiently or more effectively. Monitoring will enable problems to be identified and changes
to be made to the project in a timely way. Monitoring will also provide information that is
useful for other similar projects or for follow-on activities from the present project.
Periodic evaluations are then needed to assess how the project is meeting its objectives.
Evaluation should consist of decisions on what is to be evaluated and how the evaluation is
to be done. After the evaluation there is an analysis of the results, and their presentation to
all concerned.
15.2 Participatory Monitoring And Evaluation
Monitoring is a continuous internal activity concerned with a project and its progress;
evaluation is a periodic assessment on how a project is meeting its objectives.
A bottom up process is essential for participatory monitoring and is also desirable for
participatory evaluation.
For pragmatic reasons evaluation in project management has generally been given more
importance than monitoring, as it easily gives reliable information to the top level managers,
donor agencies, etc. However, in participatory watershed management, monitoring will be
more important as it will be used at the village level to continuously inform, measure and
motivate. Monitoring at this level will also educate. For these reasons participatory
monitoring is a powerful development tool.
Tata Energy Research Institute
Bangalore Centre



Karnataka Watershed Development Project
15-2
Monitoring & Evaluation
15.3 Participatory Monitoring
"The purpose of monitoring is to achieve effective project performance by the provision of
feedback to project management at all levels. This enables management to improve upon
operational plans and to take timely corrective action where necessary. It is an internal
activity that is an integral part of the management information system and hence an
essential part of good management practice. It is the responsibility of the management and
should be perforrmed at every level of the management hierarchy."
B.R Bhatta (1990)
For watershed management, operations will be carried out at the following levels: -
FARMER
VILLAGE
SUB WATERSHED
WATERSHED
REGION
In participatory watershed management, monitoring will begin at the farmer level and then
be aggregated at the community or village level. Alternatively it might start at the village
level and be aggregated at the next level. In either case there would then be further
aggregation until the central organisation responsible for the project has adequate
information. Thus there are up to five tiers at which information has to be presented, and a
good monitoring system will provide useful information to managers at each of these levels,
particularly the lowest.
In most cases, forestry and agricultural ministries and departments will already have a
monitoring system, and often more than one, to monitor expenditure, attendance of staff,
use of vehicles, progress against targets, etc. These systems will not have been designed to
help village people and, it will be necessary to devise a system with villagers that will meet
the needs of the lower levels in the management hierarchy.
Careful design will be needed if existing monitoring systems are to be used, and .in any case
a new system will probably prove to be more useful.
A lot of care will be necessary to design a system that can build up progressively from the
farmer or village levels. In the end it may be necessary for two monitoring systems to be in
place because there are two different objectives for the monitoring system. Put another way,
the information required to guide the villagers may be so different from the information
required to inform the upper level managers that two systems will be necessary. One of
these would work at the lowest levels suitable for all the purposes of participatory monitoring
and helping farmers, the other would be to provide information to middle and upper level
managers.
A fundamental guiding principle of good monitoring systems is that they must be simple.
Simplicity is particularly important at farmer and village levels where a simple system
showing progress can be a powerful motivator and confidence builder.
Tata Energy Research Institute
Bangalore Centre



Karnataka Watershed Development Project
15-3
Monitoring & Evaluation
Indicators are required to' measure progress made by the participants in the project, they
can also be used as an effective way of showing them what progress has been made by the
planned programmes. This requires the selection of simple indicators by all concerned -
indicators that are suggested and/or agreed upon by the insiders. The use of a few simple
but well selected quantitative indicators (e.g., expenditure against budget) can be very
effective, but the need for qualitative information should not be overlooked.
Indicators should be:
VALID
TIMELY
RELIABLE
RELEVANT
* SENSITIVE
* SPECIFIC
* COST-EFFECTIVE
* QUALITATIVE AS WELL AS QUANTITATIVE
Evaluation
Evaluation is generally defined as a periodic assessment of the performance of a project
against its stated objectives. Evaluations may be carried out during the life of the project (at
quarterly or annual intervals or at the mid-point of the project) or after the project has
ended (ex post evaluation). The evaluations that are carried out at the various times will
probably have different formats and differing degrees of intensity. Evaluation is often carried
out by persons external to the project and in these cases is not usually participatory, even
though it is easy to arrange for the evaluators to meet with village representatives or for a
community to nominate a representative to be part of the evaluation team.
In the same way that monitoring systems use indicators of progress against work
programmes, evaluations should use baselines against which a project's achievements can
be measured as a method of assessing whether a project is meeting its objectives.
15.4 Computerised Data Bases
Nowadays it is usual for monitoring systems to be computerized, using commercial data base
programmes. These make data handling more feasible and help managers who are often few
in number and overworked, to retrieve and manipulate data easily and in a cost effective
way. The project evaluation at the district and State levels can be done using data bases
collected at the other levels.
Watershed management projects are using geographic information systems more and more
e.g., by linking a GIS program with a data base program, and it is becoming possible to
store data obtained from special studies, from population censuses, monitoring and
secondary data sources in GIS data bases to be combined with other data to assist in
planning and decision-making.
Tata Energy Research Institute
Bangalore Centre



Karnataka Watershed Development Project

Environmental and Social Management Guidelines                                      16- 1
16.0 ENVIRONMENTAL AND SOCIAL MANAGEMENT GUIDELINES
7The incorporation of environmental concerns during planning, designing and monitoring
stages through the delineation of an Environmental Management Plan (EMP) will serve
as a guideline for developing project activities in the Village Development Plans and help
in enhancing positive impacts and provide guidelines for proper implementation. Each of
the Village Watershed Development Plans (VWDPs) would have an action plan with
activities derived from the consultative process and refined by the EMP. The EMP is an
important component of the REA and focuses on effective management practices and
adoptable & appropriate technologies for watershed treatment. nt also highlights the
operational practices of water harvesting structures, check dams, ponds, bench
terracing, roads etc., with recourse to minimum negative impacts. The EMP is the
outcome of the assessment of the natural resources of the region, possible project
components as well as the consultative process. This generic EMP will be refined in the
VWDPs based on the actual treatment and construction sites, in other words EMP in the
VWDP will be site specific.

*  Silt traps should be constructed along tile site before
construction commences
*  Vegetation  should  be  planted  immediately  after  the
construction work is completed
*  Borrow pits should be rehabilitated as soon as excavation
is completed
*  Signage, traffic management measures like speed breakers,
fencing, pedestrian crossings in market areas and village
centers  should  be  designed  and  integrated  in  the
construction
*  Minimum machineries to be encouraged
* Construction of Biogas plants
*  Improved smokeless chulas
*  Public Health Centers and schools to be constructed within
5 km radius
Project activities, especially those involving construction, would require certain
specifications that need to be adhered to. It is very important that proper guidelines to
carry out the activities are included in the contract documents for implementation of the
VWDP. These guidelines may be modified as and when necessary. In addition to these
guidelines, the specifications of construction practices for clearing, material sourcing,
rehabilitation of borrow areas, etc., need to be included in the contract documents to
ensure that the implementation of the activities are carried out in an environmentally
sound manner.
The construction phase itself may induce a few environmental problems during clearing
of site, material sourcing and disposal of unused construction materials. Therefore, it is
necessary to incorporate proper management measures of construction materials in the
contract document, with respect to environment. The guidelines for construction and
technical specifications for the activities involving some amount of construction work will
be provided in the VWDPs.
Tata Energy Research Institute
Bangalore Centre



Environmental and Social Management Guidelines                              16- 8
16.2 Pest Management
The production is envisaged to increase due to soil and water conservation in the project
area. Also, the development of the region could induce a change in cropping pattern and
introduction of new/exotic species. All this could result in various kinds of pest attacks.
The economic losses caused to the crops due to pest attack vary from crop to crop and
region to region. Pest attacks can be controlled through physical and mechanical control,
cultural (indigenous) control, biological methods and use of synthetic chemical pesticides
and insecticides. In the rain-fed agriculture, generally, the use of pesticides and
insecticides are low. However, commercial crops like cotton, vegetables, etc, require
application of these at critical stages to check pest attack.
Traditional measures to check pest attack exist in the region. Of these, application of
neem seed extract, cultivation of maize around vegetable garden, cultivation of marigold
with redgram, etc, are commonly practiced. Biological control is also gaining momentum.
Popularizing more and more effective indigenous pest control techniques and biological
control needs more encouragement. Awareness and capacity building regarding safe pest
management also needs importance.
Pest control through proper selection and recommended dosage of pesticides and
insecticides will result in effective Pest management, which also creates low pollution.
Department of Agriculture and State Agriculture Universities should play an active role in
Pest management through effective extension and research services through the
following activities:
*  Promoting the use of biological methods
*  Promoting the use of indigenous methods
*  Development and use of crop varieties that are resistant or tolerant to pests
*  Reduction in the reliance on synthetic chemical pesticides
*  Promoting environmental-friendly pesticides
*  Helping farmers decide on pesticides
The selection and use of any pesticide should generally follow the World Bank Policy on
Pest Management (OP 4.09).
16.3 Habitat Management
Ranebennur Sanctuary in Haveri district is the only sanctuary existing in the project
region. It is the home of the Black Buck and the Great Indian Bustard, which are rare and
endangered species. Kalledevaru Watershed in Byadgi taluk is the nearest watershed,
which is proposed to be treated in the project, at a distance of 15 km from the sanctuary.
The habitats of other species of birds, reptiles, and insects are not anticipated to be
disturbed by the activities of the proposed project. Any development of habitat
management through creation of awareness among the people and educating them on
the far-reaching implications of ecological degradation and securing their active
participation for the eco-development schemes should be part of training and capacity
building.
Due to the promotion of tree conservation, afforestation and water conservation, it is
expected that there will be enhancement in the bio-diversity of the region in terms of
both flora and fauna.
16.4 Water Conservation Interventions through the aid of Water
Budget
One of the important aspects to be considered in watershed development program,
during intervention for water conservation are the non-availability of surface water at
Tata Energy Research Institute
Bangalore Centre



Environmental and Social Management Guidelines                                    16- 9
lower reaches of watershed due to impoundments of water at upper reaches. It is also a
fact that due to this reason water conservation interventions are not taken up in the
upper reaches, which actually might result in imbalance in the development of the
watershed. In order to provide equal opportunities of development for the whole region,
it is necessary that such interventions need to consider the Water Budget of the region.
The region here is described as the watershed where the interventions are taken up. It
must be pointed out that catchment boundary need not necessarily coincide with the
groundwater basin. It is recommended that the site selection of these interventions
throughout the watershed should be planned based on the water availability and water
requirements, which is referred to as the Water Budget. The planning for location of
interventions should have the following components:
* Provisional water budget statement and calculations
* Study of development works needed to ensure adequate control and supply desired
* Comparative analysis of the various possibilities and final recommendation of the most desirable
development pattern based on principles of equity and efficiency
The water resources are surface water & ground water. The availability of water and
requirement of water should be assessed accurately. The availability of groundwater is
always limited by the rate of recharge and hence the annual recharge rate needs to be
ascertained through agencies like the Groundwater Cell of the Department of Mines &
Geology. The quantity of surface runoff also needs to be ascertained through Hydrology
studies, wherein the use of Unit Hydrographs should be developed and utilized. The
various aspects to be studied in detail, for determining the available water, are given in
the figure below

Assessment of water requirement must take into consideration, the present trend in
water requirement, and the probable social changes in the coming decades and their
effect on water requirement.

16.5 Social Management Measures
Social Management Measures should be undertaken through a Memorandum of
Understanding (MoU) between the village community and the implementing agency
(Department of Watershed Development). The MoU would incorporate the basic
principles for community responsibilities for plan preparation, implementation,
maintenance and protection of assets along with monitoring and evaluation. The MoU
essentially  incorporates  the  broad  guidelines for formation  of Micro-Watershed
Management Groups  (MWMG). The  MWMGs. will be  responsible  for effective
implementation of the VWDP as well as financial aspects. Agreements would be reached
with each MWMG on sharing of watershed treatment costs before the MoU is signed.
Apart from defining the roles and responsibilities of MWMGs, the MoU also delineates
specific social management guidelines for soil conservation, water harvesting structures,
horticulture, forestry, animal husbandry, common lands and development funds. These
MoU would form a part of the implementation plans, i.e VWDPs.
16.6 Criteria For Future Selection Of Sub-Watersheds
The criteria used for selection of sub-watersheds have been limited to five criteria given
below. The natural resources of the sub-watershed like soil characteristics, water
availability, vegetation, etc., have not been considered even though the project seeks to
address these through various activities. It is necessary, therefore, to include these as
additional criteria with weightages lower than those given for social criteria. The selection
of sub-watersheds needs to be done by an overlay of these criteria so that a spatial
dimension may be added to the selection process. The use of a Geographical Information
System is recommended for this purpose.
Each of the criteria for selection of sub-watersheds is required to be mapped at a suitable
scale and values given for their characteristics. For example, Percentage of population
Tata Energy Research Institute



Environmental and-Social Management Guidelines                             16- 11
below the poverty line may be mapped talukwise, with each taluk having values of low,
moderate or high. Natural resources may be mapped at the district level with similar
values. The willingness to cost share may have only two values of 'willing' or'not willing'.
A combination of all the criteria with the suggested weightages would give areas that
require watershed treatment. This may be done using GIS.
